<meta name="DC.Title" content="The Solanum commersonii Genome Sequence Provides Insights into Adaptation to Stress Conditions and Genome Evolution of Wild Potato Relatives" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1105/tpc.114.135954" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2015-04-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2015 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="restricted" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Here, we report the draft genome sequence of Solanum commersonii , which consists of ∼830 megabases with an N50 of 44,303 bp anchored to 12 chromosomes, using the potato ( Solanum tuberosum ) genome sequence as a reference. Compared with potato, S. commersonii shows a striking reduction in heterozygosity (1.5% versus 53 to 59%), and differences in genome sizes were mainly due to variations in intergenic sequence length. Gene annotation by ab initio prediction supported by RNA-seq data produced a catalog of 1703 predicted microRNAs, 18,882 long noncoding RNAs of which 20% are shown to target cold-responsive genes, and 39,290 protein-coding genes with a significant repertoire of nonredundant nucleotide binding site-encoding genes and 126 cold-related genes that are lacking in S. tuberosum . Phylogenetic analyses indicate that domesticated potato and S. commersonii lineages diverged ∼2.3 million years ago. Three duplication periods corresponding to genome enrichment for particular gene families related to response to salt stress, water transport, growth, and defense response were discovered. The draft genome sequence of S. commersonii substantially increases our understanding of the domesticated germplasm, facilitating translation of acquired knowledge into advances in crop stability in light of global climate and environmental changes.

*   ### Glossary
    
    EBN
    :   endosperm balance number
    CEG
    :   core eukaryotic gene
    SNP
    :   single-nucleotide polymorphism
    LTR-RT
    :   long terminal repeat-retrotranspon
    GO
    :   Gene Ontology
    ncRNA
    :   noncoding RNA
    lncRNA
    :   long noncoding RNA
    miRNA
    :   microRNA
    AC
    :   acclimated
    NAC
    :   nonacclimated
    TF
    :   transcription factor
    TE
    :   transposable element" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Here, we report the draft genome sequence of &lt;i&gt;Solanum commersonii&lt;/i&gt;, which consists of ∼830 megabases with an N50 of 44,303 bp anchored to 12 chromosomes, using the potato (&lt;i&gt;Solanum tuberosum&lt;/i&gt;) genome sequence as a reference. Compared with potato, &lt;i&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/i&gt; shows a striking reduction in heterozygosity (1.5% versus 53 to 59%), and differences in genome sizes were mainly due to variations in intergenic sequence length. Gene annotation by ab initio prediction supported by RNA-seq data produced a catalog of 1703 predicted microRNAs, 18,882 long noncoding RNAs of which 20% are shown to target cold-responsive genes, and 39,290 protein-coding genes with a significant repertoire of nonredundant nucleotide binding site-encoding genes and 126 cold-related genes that are lacking in &lt;i&gt;S. tuberosum&lt;/i&gt;. Phylogenetic analyses indicate that domesticated potato and &lt;i&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/i&gt; lineages diverged ∼2.3 million years ago. Three duplication periods corresponding to genome enrichment for particular gene families related to response to salt stress, water transport, growth, and defense response were discovered. The draft genome sequence of &lt;i&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/i&gt; substantially increases our understanding of the domesticated germplasm, facilitating translation of acquired knowledge into advances in crop stability in light of global climate and environmental changes.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="The Solanum commersonii Genome Sequence Provides Insights into Adaptation to Stress Conditions and Genome Evolution of Wild Potato Relatives" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/27/4.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Here, we report the draft genome sequence of Solanum commersonii , which consists of ∼830 megabases with an N50 of 44,303 bp anchored to 12 chromosomes, using the potato ( Solanum tuberosum ) genome sequence as a reference. Compared with potato, S. commersonii shows a striking reduction in heterozygosity (1.5% versus 53 to 59%), and differences in genome sizes were mainly due to variations in intergenic sequence length. Gene annotation by ab initio prediction supported by RNA-seq data produced a catalog of 1703 predicted microRNAs, 18,882 long noncoding RNAs of which 20% are shown to target cold-responsive genes, and 39,290 protein-coding genes with a significant repertoire of nonredundant nucleotide binding site-encoding genes and 126 cold-related genes that are lacking in S. tuberosum . Phylogenetic analyses indicate that domesticated potato and S. commersonii lineages diverged ∼2.3 million years ago. Three duplication periods corresponding to genome enrichment for particular gene families related to response to salt stress, water transport, growth, and defense response were discovered. The draft genome sequence of S. commersonii substantially increases our understanding of the domesticated germplasm, facilitating translation of acquired knowledge into advances in crop stability in light of global climate and environmental changes.

*   ### Glossary
    
    EBN
    :   endosperm balance number
    CEG
    :   core eukaryotic gene
    SNP
    :   single-nucleotide polymorphism
    LTR-RT
    :   long terminal repeat-retrotranspon
    GO
    :   Gene Ontology
    ncRNA
    :   noncoding RNA
    lncRNA
    :   long noncoding RNA
    miRNA
    :   microRNA
    AC
    :   acclimated
    NAC
    :   nonacclimated
    TF
    :   transcription factor
    TE
    :   transposable element" />

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2015 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-4">Here, we report the draft genome sequence of <em>Solanum commersonii</em>, which consists of ∼830 megabases with an N50 of 44,303 bp anchored to 12 chromosomes, using the potato (<em>Solanum tuberosum</em>) genome sequence as a reference. Compared with potato, <em>S. commersonii</em> shows a striking reduction in heterozygosity (1.5% versus 53 to 59%), and differences in genome sizes were mainly due to variations in intergenic sequence length. Gene annotation by ab initio prediction supported by RNA-seq data produced a catalog of 1703 predicted microRNAs, 18,882 long noncoding RNAs of which 20% are shown to target cold-responsive genes, and 39,290 protein-coding genes with a significant repertoire of nonredundant nucleotide binding site-encoding genes and 126 cold-related genes that are lacking in <em>S. tuberosum</em>. Phylogenetic analyses indicate that domesticated potato and <em>S. commersonii</em> lineages diverged ∼2.3 million years ago. Three duplication periods corresponding to genome enrichment for particular gene families related to response to salt stress, water transport, growth, and defense response were discovered. The draft genome sequence of <em>S. commersonii</em> substantially increases our understanding of the domesticated germplasm, facilitating translation of acquired knowledge into advances in crop stability in light of global climate and environmental changes.</p></div><div class="section introduction" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">INTRODUCTION</h2><p id="p-17">The genus <em>Solanum</em> ranks among the largest of plant genera and includes several cultivated crops of regional or worldwide significance including potato (<em>Solanum tuberosum</em>) and tomato (<em>Solanum lycopersicum</em>). Potato is the most important non-cereal food crop worldwide, offering higher yields in calories per acre than any grain (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Bradeen and Haynes, 2011</a>). Unfortunately, potato is also host to a wide variety of pathogens of large economic impact, including fungi, oomycetes, bacteria, viruses, and nematodes (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Stevenson et al., 2001</a>). In addition, abiotic stress factors such as cold, heat, drought, and salinity have a significant effect on cultivated potato, affecting yield, tuber quality, and market value (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Wang-Pruski and Schofield, 2012</a>). To improve resistance to these adverse environmental factors, potato breeders can exploit the ∼200 tuber-bearing <em>Solanum</em> species native to South, Central, and North America. Although these genetic resources provide great potential to breed improved cultivars (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Huamán et al., 2000</a>), linkage drag typically limits the use of wild potato species since many exotic genes imparting undesirable traits (e.g., high alkaloid content, long stolons, etc.) can be cotransferred with desirable genes (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Bradshaw and Ramsay, 2005</a>).</p><p id="p-18"><em>Solanum commersonii</em> is a tuber-bearing wild potato species native to Central and South America. The French taxonomist Michel-Felix Dunal named this species in honor of Philibert Commerson (1727–1773), who collected the type specimen (No. 47) in 1767 at Montevideo, Uruguay. This was probably the first wild potato to be collected on a scientific expedition (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Hawkes, 1990</a>). Analyses of chloroplast genome restriction sites and nitrate reductase gene sequence confirmed that <em>S. commersonii</em> is phylogenetically distinct from cultivated potato (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Rodríguez and Spooner, 2009</a>). Consistent with these analyses, <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> are sexually incompatible and have been assigned different endosperm balance numbers (<a id="xref-def-1-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">EBNs</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Johnston et al., 1980</a>), with <em>S. commersonii</em> reported as 1 <a id="xref-def-1-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">EBN</a> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> reported as 4 <a id="xref-def-1-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">EBN</a>. Despite being genetically isolated from cultivated potato, <em>S. commersonii</em> has garnered significant research interest. It possesses several resistance traits not found in cultivated potato, including resistance to root knot nematode, soft rot and blackleg, bacterial and verticillium wilt, <em>Potato virus X</em>, tobacco etch virus, common scab, and late blight (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Hanneman and Bamberg, 1986</a>; <a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Hawkes, 1990</a>; <a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Micheletto et al., 2000</a>). Particularly attractive is its freezing tolerance and capacity to cold acclimate (i.e., ability to increase cold tolerance after exposure to low, nonfreezing temperatures). By contrast, the cultivated potato is classified as sensitive to low temperatures and is unable to cold acclimate (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Palta and Simon, 1993</a>). Both frost tolerance and cold acclimation capacity are important breeding traits, since temperatures below 0°C are a major cause of yield losses in several production regions. Using various strategies, breeders have successfully overcome sexual isolation barriers to introgress <em>S. commersonii</em> genetic material into cultivated potato (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Cardi et al., 1993</a>; <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Bamberg et al., 1994</a>; <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Carputo et al., 1997</a>). Despite such efforts, very little progress has been made in the release of new varieties originating from crosses involving <em>S. commersonii</em>. This is at least partially due to the lack of genomics resources for <em>S. commersonii</em> and other potato wild relatives. The genome sequence of the cultivated potato was published in 2011 (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium, 2011</a>), ushering in a new era of potato functional and comparative genomics. By contrast, no genome sequence of a wild potato species has been published to date. Comparative sequencing of cultivated potato and wild relatives will increase the use of wild species for crop breeding and improvement.</p><p id="p-19">Here, we describe draft genome and transcriptome sequences of the wild potato species <em>S. commersonii</em>. We used Illumina technology to sequence and de novo assemble the genome of the cold-tolerant accession PI 243503, obtaining roughly 105x coverage. The genome sequence consists of ∼830 Mb, of which 44.5% comprises transposable elements. Gene annotation by ab initio prediction supported by RNA-seq data led to the identification of 39,290 protein-coding genes with a significant repertoire of nonredundant nucleotide binding site-encoding genes and 126 cold-related genes, which lack orthologs in <em>S. tuberosum</em>. Phylogenetic analyses offered new insights into recent duplications and divergence between <em>S. commersonii</em> and the domesticated potato. Overall, the draft genome sequence of <em>S. commersonii</em> provides an essential reference for studying <em>Solanum</em> diversity via resequencing of additional wild species genomes.</p></div><div class="section results" id="sec-2"><h2 class="">RESULTS</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing and Assembling</h3><p id="p-20">To obtain a whole-genome shotgun sequence assembly of <em>S. commersonii</em> clone cmm1t, we produced size-selected sequencing paired-end and mate pair libraries based on six insert sizes ranging from 400 bp to 10 kb (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 1</a>). A total of 145.93 Gb of sequence reads was produced. After filtering low-quality sequences, the remaining 88 Gb were assembled into 278,460 contigs with an N50 contig length of 6506 bp (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). All contigs were further assembled into 64,665 scaffolds (&gt;1 kb), of which 4833 containing 50% of the assembly were 44.3 kb or larger (N50 = 44,298 kb; <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 2</a>). Employing an interactive mapping approach using the potato genome as a reference, the <em>S. commersonii</em> scaffolds were anchored onto each chromosome, resulting in 12 pseudomolecules representing the <em>S. commersonii</em> scaffolds linked and ordered according to homology with <em>S. tuberosum</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 1</a>). The <em>S. commersonii</em> genome size was measured at ∼830 Mb by flow cytometry (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 2</a>), consistent with genome size estimation (838 Mb) via 23-nucleotide depth distribution (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 3</a>). The sum of the Illumina sequences obtained represented ∼105x coverage (filtered reads) of the <em>S. commersonii</em> nuclear genome. Gaps within scaffolds ranged in length from 1 to 8369 bp, with a median length of 213 bp (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 4</a>). The GC content within <em>S. commersonii</em> coding DNA sequence was 34.5% (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 3</a>). To assess the proportion of the gene space captured in this draft genome assembly, we aligned 248 sequences from the nonredundant core eukaryotic genes (<a id="xref-def-2-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">CEGs</a>) to the genome assembly. In total, 243 (98%) <a id="xref-def-2-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">CEGs</a> homologs were found in the <em>S. commersonii</em> genome, suggesting that the assembly captured a large majority of the gene space (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 5</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/585/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/585/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <span class="caption-title">Metrics of <em>S. commersonii</em> Genome Assembly</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Genomic Variations</h3><p id="p-21">Compared with the cultivated potato, the <em>S. commersonii</em> genome showed a lower level of heterozygosity (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Hirsch et al., 2013</a>). A total of 9,894,571 reliable single-nucleotide polymorphisms (<a id="xref-def-3-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a>) were identified among 662,040,919 reliable genome bases (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 4</a>), yielding a <a id="xref-def-3-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNP</a> frequency of 1.49%. We evaluated the structural and functional effect of <a id="xref-def-3-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a>. Of all <a id="xref-def-3-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a>, 92% had a distance of &lt;50 bp to the nearest neighboring <a id="xref-def-3-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNP</a>. Overall 12,412 genes encompassed <a id="xref-def-3-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a>, of which 11,608 had a <a id="xref-def-3-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNP</a> rate of &lt;1% (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 6</a>). With regard to functional annotation, most of the identified <a id="xref-def-3-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a> (84%) were located in intergenic regions (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 5</a>). The 12,412 <a id="xref-def-3-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNP</a>-containing genes displayed overrepresentation of some major functional categories, including macromolecule metabolic processes, response to stimuli, carbohydrate derivative binding, localization, and ion binding (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 1</a>). The genome size difference between <em>S. commersonii</em> (830 Mb) and the doubled monoploid clone DM1-3 516 R44 of <em>S. tuberosum</em> (838 Mb) was mainly due to differences in intergenic sequence length (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1</a>). The results of microsynteny analyses revealed greater frequency of <a id="xref-def-3-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a> and insertion-deletion events (indels) spanning intergenic regions, consistent with this observation. Roughly 383 Mb of repetitive sequences were identified, accounting for 44.5% of the current assembly of the <em>S. commersonii</em> genome (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2A</a>). Analysis of kmer distribution in unassembled reads estimated 51.3% of the genome as nonrepetitive (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Liu et al., 2012</a>), and graph-based clustering with RepeatExplorer (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Novák et al., 2013</a>) detected a fraction of repeated sequences equal to ∼36% of the total genome. Although these data are not conclusive, they suggest that, compared with potato (<a id="xref-ref-47-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium, 2011</a>), <em>S. commersonii</em> might have a lower amount of repetitive DNA (44.5% versus 55%), which might predict different genome dynamics in these two species since their separation from a common ancestor. The repetitive fraction of the <em>S. commersonii</em> genome assembly is dominated by long terminal repeat-retrotranspons (<a id="xref-def-4-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">LTR-RTs</a>) (∼34%) with lower levels of several other repeat types (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2B</a>). Characterization of SINE families allowed annotating 1925 SINEs with significant similarity to families previously described in <em>S. tuberosum</em> and in other <em>Solanaceae</em> (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Wenke et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Seibt et al., 2012</a>) (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 6</a>).</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div
