<em>S. commersonii.</em> </p><p id="p-27"><strong>(B)</strong> Comparison of transposable element lengths between <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum.</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Annotation</h3><p id="p-28">Gene prediction was performed by combining results obtained from ab initio prediction, homology searches, and experimental support (cmm ESTs). The de novo assembled transcriptome encompassed ∼96% of all predicted <em>S. commersonii</em> genes (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 7</a> and <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 7</a>). Results for the functional annotation of the <em>S. commersonii</em> transcripts are reported in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figures 8A to 8C</a>. Fewer genes (37,662; annotation evidence distance ≤ 0.5) were predicted in <em>S. commersonii</em> than in potato (∼39,000), but the wild species has more predicted genes than tomato (34,727). Of predicted <em>S. commersonii</em> genes, 30,477 predicted protein-coding genes had significant BLAST similarity to protein-coding genes from other organisms in the nonredundant NCBI database. Nearly 20,500 <em>S. commersonii</em> genes were assigned to Gene Ontology (<a id="xref-def-5-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a>) terms, and more than 4900 proteins were annotated with a four-digit EC number. These data implied that more than 24% of the predicted proteome of <em>S. commersonii</em> has enzymatic function. A large number of transcripts (20,994) with no apparent coding capacity were predicted in <em>S. commersonii</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 8D</a>). These noncoding RNAs (<a id="xref-def-6-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ncRNAs</a>) comprised a diverse group of transcripts, including 22 tRNAs, 40 rRNAs, 18,882 long noncoding RNAs (<a id="xref-def-7-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">lncRNAs</a>), and 1703 putative microRNA (<a id="xref-def-8-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a>) precursors. Among the latter, 360 were predicted to fold into a secondary structure leading to the typical <a id="xref-def-8-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a>/<a id="xref-def-8-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a>* double-stranded RNA duplexes (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 2</a>). In addition, 47 of these transcripts showed similarity to known mature <a id="xref-def-8-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNAs</a> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Tables 8 and 9</a>). A key step toward understanding the biological functions of the predicted <a id="xref-def-8-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNAs</a> was achieved through the identification of 4437 target sites. According to <a id="xref-def-5-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a> term classification, 22% (976) of the target genes are involved in cold response (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 8</a>) and 10 are potential regulators of transcripts annotated as responsive to cold (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 10</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Analysis and Genome Evolution</h3><p id="p-29">To gain insight into the evolution of the <em>S. commersonii</em> genome, we compared its virtual proteome with predicted proteins of 11 other fully sequenced plant genomes (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 11</a>), including <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. lycopersicum</em> (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a>). The resulting 35,182 phylogenetic trees, available through PhylomeDB (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Huerta-Cepas et al., 2014</a>), were scanned to predict phylogeny-based orthology and paralogy relationships (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Gabaldón, 2008</a>), detect and date duplication events (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Huerta-Cepas and Gabaldón, 2011</a>), and transfer annotations to <em>S. commersonii</em> genes from their functionally characterized one-to-one orthologs (<a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Huerta-Cepas and Gabaldón, 2011</a>). Roughly 17,300 (44%) and 16,821 (42%) <em>S. commersonii</em> genes showed one-to-one orthology with genes from <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. lycopersicum</em>, respectively, but only 7058 (18%) with genes from the more distantly related asterid <em>Mimulus guttatus</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 12</a>). Out of 35,182 phylogenies obtained, 9445 (24%), 7316 (21%), and 14,061 (40%) showed at least one duplication event at the <em>S. commersonii</em>, potato ancestor, and <em>Solanum</em> ancestor nodes, respectively, compared with only 1814 trees (5%) showing a duplication at the base of asterids (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 13</a>). The overall average number of duplications per branch (duplication density) was 0.66, 0.93, and 0.94 for <em>S. commersonii</em>, potato ancestor, and <em>Solanum</em> ancestor, respectively, whereas we found a low rate of 0.066 for the common ancestor of asterids (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 13</a>). To gain further insight into the divergence between <em>S. commersonii</em> and the domesticated potato, we measured transversions at 4-fold degenerate sites (4DTv) for orthologous gene pairs between <em>S. commersonii</em> and either the domesticated potato or tomato (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>) and between paralogous gene pairs diverged from duplications at each of the three relevant duplication periods investigated. Substitution rates at orthologous sites between <em>S. commersonii</em> and tomato peaked at 0.225, whereas those between <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> at 0.077. Assuming a divergence time between tomato and potato of 7.3 million years (<a id="xref-ref-47-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium, 2011</a>), and a constant mutation rate between the three lineages, this renders an estimate of ∼2.3 million years for the separation of domesticated potato and <em>S. commersonii</em> lineages. The analysis of the paralogous pairs revealed at all three relative ages showed a similar pattern, with the two most prominent peaks largely preceding the divergence of <em>S. commersonii</em> and tomato. Paralogous genes mapped to the <em>S. commersonii</em>-specific duplication (age 1) did show an additional, younger peak at 4DTv values. We assessed the genomic organization of these recent duplicates and found that most of them were present in tandem (314) or at least closely associated in the same contig (141). Finally, we assessed functional enrichment among gene families duplicated at each of these three periods (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 3</a>). Response to salt stress and water transport were terms found to be enriched exclusively among <em>S. commersonii</em> specific duplications. The ancestral potato duplication was enriched in terms related to cadmium, metal ion binding, or synthesis of terpenes, whereas terms related to nitrogen starvation, response to ethylene, response to gamma radiation, and maltose metabolism were enriched in the duplications preceding the common <em>Solanum</em> ancestor. All three duplication periods shared enrichments in defense response and growth. Transposon-related terms were enriched in the largest expanded families in <em>S. commersonii</em>, indicating active expansion of transposons (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 14</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic Relationships of 12 Sequenced Plant Species and Comparative Protein Sequence Analysis.Species tree based on maximum likelihood analysis of a concatenated alignment of 454 widespread single-copy protein sequences. Different background colors indicate taxonomic groupings within the species used to make the tree. Inset square highlights the species belonging to the genus Solanum. Bars represent the total number of genes for each species (scale on the top). Bars are divided to indicate different types of homology relationships: dark green, widespread genes that are found in at least 11 of the 12 species; orange, widespread but asterid-specific genes that are found in at least three of the four asterid species; gray, species-specific genes with no (detectable) homologs in other species; brown, genes without a clear homology pattern. The thin purple line under each bar represents the percentage of genes with at least one paralog in a given species. The thin dark-gray line represents the percentage of S. commersonii genes that have homologs in a given species." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-527814768" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Phylogenetic Relationships of 12 Sequenced Plant Species and Comparative Protein Sequence Analysis.Species tree based on maximum likelihood analysis of a concatenated alignment of 454 widespread single-copy protein sequences. Different background colors indicate taxonomic groupings within the species used to make the tree. Inset square highlights the species belonging to the genus &lt;em&gt;Solanum&lt;/em&gt;. Bars represent the total number of genes for each species (scale on the top). Bars are divided to indicate different types of homology relationships: dark green, widespread genes that are found in at least 11 of the 12 species; orange, widespread but asterid-specific genes that are found in at least three of the four asterid species; gray, species-specific genes with no (detectable) homologs in other species; brown, genes without a clear homology pattern. The thin purple line under each bar represents the percentage of genes with at least one paralog in a given species. The thin dark-gray line represents the percentage of &lt;em&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/em&gt; genes that have homologs in a given species.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="153"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="153"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/760" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3.</span> <p id="p-30" class="first-child">Phylogenetic Relationships of 12 Sequenced Plant Species and Comparative Protein Sequence Analysis.</p><p id="p-31">Species tree based on maximum likelihood analysis of a concatenated alignment of 454 widespread single-copy protein sequences. Different background colors indicate taxonomic groupings within the species used to make the tree. Inset square highlights the species belonging to the genus <em>Solanum</em>. Bars represent the total number of genes for each species (scale on the top). Bars are divided to indicate different types of homology relationships: dark green, widespread genes that are found in at least 11 of the 12 species; orange, widespread but asterid-specific genes that are found in at least three of the four asterid species; gray, species-specific genes with no (detectable) homologs in other species; brown, genes without a clear homology pattern. The thin purple line under each bar represents the percentage of genes with at least one paralog in a given species. The thin dark-gray line represents the percentage of <em>S. commersonii</em> genes that have homologs in a given species.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Major Evolutionary Events Related to the Evolution of S. commersonii as Revealed by the 4DTv Analysis.Two speciation events at the split of ancestral potatoes and tomatoes (cyan line peak) and the split between S. tuberosum and S. commersonii (magenta line peak). No recent whole-genome duplication event was detected specifically for S. commersonii. Previously proposed ancient whole-genome duplication events were confirmed using paralogous genes mapped to three different relative evolutionary time points." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-527814768" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Major Evolutionary Events Related to the Evolution of &lt;em&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/em&gt; as Revealed by the 4DTv Analysis.Two speciation events at the split of ancestral potatoes and tomatoes (cyan line peak) and the split between &lt;em&gt;S. tuberosum&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/em&gt; (magenta line peak). No recent whole-genome duplication event was detected specifically for &lt;em&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/em&gt;. Previously proposed ancient whole-genome duplication events were confirmed using paralogous genes mapped to three different relative evolutionary time points.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="246"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 4." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="246"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/588" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 4.</span> <p id="p-32" class="first-child">Major Evolutionary Events Related to the Evolution of <em>S. commersonii</em> as Revealed by the 4DTv Analysis.</p><p id="p-33">Two speciation events at the split of ancestral potatoes and tomatoes (cyan line peak) and the split between <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. commersonii</em> (magenta line peak). No recent whole-genome duplication event was detected specifically for <em>S. commersonii</em>. Previously proposed ancient whole-genome duplication events were confirmed using paralogous genes mapped to three different relative evolutionary time points.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Pathogen-Receptor Gene Annotation</h3><p id="p-34">A catalog of 942 and 1406 nonredundant pathogen recognition proteins was created from the <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> proteomes, respectively. We classified the corresponding genes into various structural categories based on the arrangement of encoded domains (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). In <em>S. commersonii</em>, 286 coiled-coil-nucleotide binding site (NBS)-leucine-rich repeat (LRR) (CNL), 71 NBS, 143 Toll/interleukin-1 receptor (TIR)-NBS, and 37 TIR genes were found. More than 250 receptor-like kinases and 280 receptor-like proteins (RLPs) were also recorded. In comparison, in <em>S. tuberosum</em>, 506 CNL, 199 NBS, 199 TIR-NBS, 36 TIR, 313 receptor-like kinase, and 237 RLP genes were identified. The <em>S. tuberosum</em> genome also includes 14 TIR-LRR genes. Previously, using similar approaches, we cataloged the pathogen recognition proteins from tomato (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Andolfo et al., 2014a</a>, <a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2014b</a>). While the <em>S. tuberosum</em> genome contains nearly twice as many CNL genes as <em>S. commersonii</em> (506 versus 286, respectively), the tomato genome contains roughly half as many CNL genes as <em>S. commersonii</em> (81 versus 186, respectively). By contrast, <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> encode a larger complement of TIR-NBS-LRR and RLP proteins than does tomato. These findings suggest that the pathogen receptor gene repertoire in each <em>Solanum</em> species is uniquely shaped based on pathogen pressures and life history.</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/560/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/560/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2.</span> <span class="caption-title">Numbers of <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> Genes Encoding Proteins with Domains Similar to Those Found in Plant Pathogen Receptor Proteins</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-36">Syntenic relationships between pathogen receptor genes in <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> were further explored by comparative analysis of three loci involved in <em>Phytophthora infestans</em> resistance, <em>Rpi-blb2</em> (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">van der Vossen et al., 2005</a>), <em>Tm-2</em> (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Lanfermeijer et al., 2003</a>), and <em>R1</em> (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Ballvora et al., 2002</a>). All are members of the CNL superfamily and all exist within clusters of related gene copies. In <em>S. tuberosum, Rpi-blb2</em> is part of a 15-gene cluster (<a id="xref-ref-60-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">van der Vossen et al., 2005</a>), but in <em>S. commersonii</em>, only four corresponding gene copies were present. Similarly, in <em>S. tuberosum,</em> the <em>Tm-2</em> cluster comprises four gene copies (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Lanfermeijer et al., 2003</a>), while in <em>S. commersonii</em>, only two genes were annotated. For <em>R1</em>, there was clear variation between the species in terms of the physical cluster size and number of genes included. The <em>S. tuberosum R1</em> cluster was longer than that of <em>S. commersonii</em> (300 kb versus 37 kb, respectively) and comprised more gene copies. The <em>S. commersonii</em> genome contains three <em>R1</em> genes with clear orthologous relationships to genes in <em>S. tuberosum</em>. Specifically, <em>S. commersonii R1B-23-like</em>, <em>R1C-3-like</em>, and <em>R1A-4-like</em> correspond to <em>S. tuberosum R1B-23</em>, <em>R1C-3</em>, and <em>R1A-4</em>, respectively. These three <em>S. commersonii R1</em> orthologous pairs exhibited an average nucleotide identity of 93% but were arranged in reverse order in the two species (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9</a>). Furthermore, additional unrelated genes found in the <em>S. tuberosum R1B-23</em> to <em>R1C-3</em> interval and <em>R1C-3</em> and <em>R1A-4</em> interval were completely absent in the <em>S. commersonii R1</em> gene cluster. Comparison of <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> orthologous <em>R1</em> gene copies revealed further substantial structural variation in some cases. In particular, while the coding sequence lengths of <em>R1B-23</em> and <em>R1A-4</em> were similar for <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. commersonii</em> genes, <em>S. tuberosum R1C-3</em> was only about one-third the size of <em>S. commersonii R1C-3</em> (4207 bp versus 12,386 bp, respectively). Differences in the number of exons and introns between <em>R1</em> orthologs were also found (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Cold-Responsive Gene Analysis</h3><p id="p-37">A total of 5853 and 8666 predicted protein sequences similar to <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> proteins annotated as responsive to cold were identified in <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em>, respectively (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 10</a>). In <em>S. commersonii</em>, 1451 proteins were homologous to Arabidopsis sequences annotated with the <a id="xref-def-5-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a> term <em>cold acclimation</em> (hereinafter CA-like), 257 with the term <em>cellular response to cold</em> (hereinafter CRC-like), and 4145 with the term <em>response to cold</em> (hereinafter RC-like). In <em>S. tuberosum</em>, 2199 were in the CA-like group, 362 in the CRC-like group, and 6105 in the RC-like group. Enriched <a id="xref-def-5-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a> term categories were found in both species (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 10A</a> and <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Sets 4 and 5</a>). Roughly 2860 genes were in these categories in <em>S. commersonii</em> (707, 85, and 2072 belonging to the CA-like, CRC-like, and RC-like groups, respectively). By contrast only 532, 181, and 1539 genes were assigned to those categories in <em>S. tuberosum</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figures 10B and 10C</a>). <a id="xref-def-5-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a> annotation also revealed that 34 CA-like, CRC-like, and RC-like categories encompassed a large number (1546) of cold-responsive genes harboring a <a id="xref-def-3-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNP</a> (12.4% of total) (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 10D</a> and <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 6</a>). In addition, out of 126 unique annotated <em>S. commersonii</em> genes involved in response to cold, 32 belonged to CA-like, 4 to CRTC-like, and 90 to RTC-like <a id="xref-def-5-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a> terms (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 10E</a>).</p><p id="p-38">To identify genes involved in freezing and cold acclimation responses, the transcript expression profiles of frost stress acclimated (<a id="xref-def-9-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a>) and nonacclimated (<a id="xref-def-10-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>) plants were compared with that of non-frost-stressed plants grown at 24°C. We identified 855 differentially expressed genes: 720 under <a id="xref-def-9-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions and 784 under <a id="xref-def-10-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions. Venn diagram analysis indicated that 71 genes were differently expressed (mostly upregulated) exclusively under <a id="xref-def-9-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions and 135 only under <a id="xref-def-10-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions. Roughly 650 genes were found responsive to both conditions (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 11</a> and <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 7</a>). Different functional categories appeared to be enriched under either <a id="xref-def-9-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> or <a id="xref-def-10-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 15</a>). Among <a id="xref-def-10-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> differentially expressed genes, the most significantly enriched groups were those involved in cytoplasmic part (GO:0044444), organelle metabolisms (GO:0044422 and GO:0043226), and in phytosteroid and brassinosteroid metabolic processes (GO:0016128 and GO:0016131, respectively). For <a id="xref-def-9-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> differentially expressed genes, the most represented <a id="xref-def-5-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">GO</a> terms were in response to metal and cadmium ions (GO:0010038 and GO:0046686) symplast (GO:0055044) and vacuolar part (GO:0044437). Protein kinases and phosphatases with altered expression under <a id="xref-def-10-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and <a id="xref-def-9-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions were among the most differentially expressed groups (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 7</a>). In addition, proteins involved in the cold response machinery, such as antioxidant cascades, secondary metabolism, cell wall polysaccharide remodeling, starch metabolism, and protein folding (heat shock protein 70 [HSP70]), were found (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 6</a>). Out of 855 cold differentially expressed genes, 56 (6.5%) were annotated as transcription factors (<a id="xref-def-11-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a>) with known DNA binding domains (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 12</a>). Thirty-eight <a id="xref-def-11-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a> were differentially expressed under both <a id="xref-def-9-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and <a id="xref-def-10-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions, 15 only under <a id="xref-def-10-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>, and 3 exclusively under <a id="xref-def-9-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a>. The apetala 2/ethylene-response element binding factor (AP2/ERF) domain was the most represented <a id="xref-def-11-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TF</a> family, accounting for 17 differently expressed <a id="xref-def-11-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a>.</p><p id="p-39">A set of 19 genes encoding cold-sensing and signaling proteins was specifically analyzed under both <a id="xref-def-10-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and <a id="xref-def-9-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5A</a>). The cold acclimation pathway is initiated when plants sense low temperatures through membrane rigidification, triggering a surge of Ca<sup>2+</sup> into the cytosol. Plants possess groups of Ca<sup>2+</sup> sensors, including CDPKs (Ca<sup>2+</sup>-dependent protein kinases), CBL (calcineurin B-like protein), and CIPKs (CBL interacting protein kinase). In <em>S. commersonii</em>, the expression of <em>CDPK7</em>, <em>-17</em>, and <em>-19</em>, as well as <em>CIPK1</em>, <em>-3</em>, and <em>-23</em>, was profiled under both <a id="xref-def-9-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and <a id="xref-def-10-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions. Transcription of <em>CDPK7</em>, <em>CIPK3</em>, and <em>CIPK23</em> was activated under <a id="xref-def-10-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and <a id="xref-def-9-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions. By contrast, transcription of <em>CIPK1</em> was suppressed under <a id="xref-def-10-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and transcription of <em>CDPK17</em> and <em>-19</em> was not affected by acclimation.</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Cold-Responsive Gene Expression Analysis.(A) Cold-sensing and signaling pathway and gene expression heat map. Expression levels are indicated by shades of blue (downregulation) and red (upregulation), whereas white indicates no differences between control and stressed plants (for AC or NAC plants). PM, plasma membrane; NM, nuclear membrane.(B) Structural organization of the S. tuberosum and S. commersonii CBF regions.(C) Similarity tree of the Solanum CBFs in relation to the Arabidopsis, Brassica, and Triticum polypeptides having the CBF signature sequences. The bar indicates branch length scale." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-527814768" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Cold-Responsive Gene Expression Analysis.&lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Cold-sensing and signaling pathway and gene expression heat map. Expression levels are indicated by shades of blue (downregulation) and red (upregulation), whereas white indicates no differences between control and stressed plants (for AC or NAC plants). PM, plasma membrane; NM, nuclear membrane.&lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Structural organization of the &lt;em&gt;S. tuberosum&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;S. commersonii&lt;/em&gt; CBF regions.&lt;strong&gt;(C)&lt;/strong&gt; Similarity tree of the &lt;em&gt;Solanum&lt;/em&gt; CBFs in relation to the Arabidopsis, &lt;em&gt;Brassica&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;Triticum&lt;/em&gt; polypeptides having the CBF signature sequences. The bar indicates branch length scale.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F5.medium.gif" width="344" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 5." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F5.medium.gif" width="344" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/27/4/954/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/606" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5.</span> <p id="p-40" class="first-child">Cold-Responsive Gene Expression Analysis.</p><p id="p-41"><strong>(A)</strong> Cold-sensing and signaling pathway and gene expression heat map. Expression levels are indicated by shades of blue (downregulation) and red (upregulation), whereas white indicates no differences between control and stressed plants (for <a id="xref-def-9-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> or <a id="xref-def-10-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> plants). PM, plasma membrane; NM, nuclear membrane.</p><p id="p-42"><strong>(B)</strong> Structural organization of the <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. commersonii</em> CBF regions.</p><p id="p-43"><strong>(C)</strong> Similarity tree of the <em>Solanum</em> CBFs in relation to the Arabidopsis, <em>Brassica</em>, and <em>Triticum</em> polypeptides having the CBF signature sequences. The bar indicates branch length scale.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-44">Inside the nucleus, the low-temperature signal transduction pathway triggers the expression of C-repeat binding factor (CBF) genes and their upstream regulators, namely, <em>ICE1</em> (<em>inducer of CBF expression</em>), a positive regulator of <em>CBF3</em>, <em>HOS1</em> (<em>high expression of osmotically sensitive</em>), a negative regulator of <em>ICE1</em>, and <em>SIZ1</em>, a <em>SUMO E3</em> ligase, which mediates sumoylation (SUMO conjugation) of <em>ICE1</em>. In <em>S. commersonii</em>, we found <em>ICE1</em> transcription was suppressed under both conditions tested, whereas <em>HOS1</em> and <em>SIZ1</em> expression was consistently downregulated under both <a id="xref-def-10-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and <a id="xref-def-9-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a>. In light of their prominent role in plant cold acclimation, we also examined <em>S. commersonii</em> CBF gene structural organization and surveyed gene expression patterns under <a id="xref-def-9-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and <a id="xref-def-10-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions. In total, we identified four <em>S. commersonii</em> CBFs (<em>CBF1</em>, <em>-2</em>, <em>-3</em>, and <em>-4</em>) and pseudogenes of CBF2 (<em>ψCBF2</em>) and CBF3 (<em>ψCBF3</em>). <em>S. commersonii</em> CBFs were collinear with <em>S. tuberosum</em> CBFs, although an <em>S. tuberosum CBF5</em> ortholog was missing in <em>S. commersonii</em>. Structural variant analysis revealed the presence of few insertions and/or deletions (indels) within the coding sequences analyzed. By contrast, low conservation of <em>CBF2</em> and <em>CBF4</em> upstream regions was observed (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5B</a>). The <em>S. commersonii ψCBF2</em> possessed only portions of a coding sequence with numerous nonsense codons in all reading frames (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 13A</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Pennycooke et al., 2008</a>). The duplicated <em>S. commersonii CBF3</em> encoded the amino acid block ASP-ALA-SER-TRY-ARG (hereafter DASWR) positioned immediately downstream from the 60-amino-acid AP2/ERF DNA binding domain in the CBF protein. However, it lacked the PKKPAGR sequence positioned upstream of the domain (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 13B</a>). Phylogenetic analysis showed that <em>S. commersonii CBF2</em> and <em>CBF3</em> grouped with <em>S. tuberosum</em> CBF sequences (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5C</a>), supporting robust orthology relationships. By contrast, <em>S. commersonii CBF1</em> was independent of the <em>Solanum</em> CBF1 clade, probably due to poorly conserved sequences flanking the AP2/ERF domain (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 13C</a>). We wondered whether the <em>S. commersonii</em> CBF transcripts accumulated differently under <a id="xref-def-10-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and <a id="xref-def-9-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions. Interestingly, we found that all <em>S. commersonii</em> CBF genes were highly responsive, regardless of whether the plant experienced acclimation, with <em>CBF1</em> and <em>CBF3</em> being most actively responsive (<a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5A</a>).</p><p id="p-45">CBFs can activate expression of a battery of downstream target genes, also called CBF regulon genes, by binding CRT/DRE elements in target promoter regions. Among these, cold responsive (COR) genes act in concert to enhance freezing tolerance. In <em>S. commersonii</em>, COR genes responded differently to the cold stimulus depending on whether the plants were first acclimated. In particular, <em>COR47</em> and <em>COR78</em> were upregulated under both <a id="xref-def-9-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and <a id="xref-def-10-17" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions. By contrast, <em>COR15a</em> and <em>COR413</em> were upregulated under <a id="xref-def-9-17" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> but downregulated under <a id="xref-def-10-18" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>. In Arabidopsis, different components of the histone acetyltransferase complex were described to interact with CBF1 in vitro and are needed for CBF1 function (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Stockinger et al., 2001</a>). Therefore we checked the expression of <em>S. commersonii ADA2b</em> and <em>GCN5</em>, two components of histone acetyltransferase complex. Our data showed that both <em>Ada2b</em> and <em>GCN5</em> were transcribed whether or not acclimation occurred (<a id="xref-fig-5-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5A</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-9"><h2 class="">DISCUSSION</h2><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing, Assembly, and Gene Annotation</h3><p id="p-46">In this work, we de novo sequenced the genome of stress-tolerant <em>S. commersonii</em> as an integral step toward deciphering the genetic bases of agricultural traits that can be improved using genes from this wild germplasm donor. The resulting <em>S. commersonii</em> genome assembly is comparable in length to the reference <em>S. tuberosum</em> genome, but divergence between the two sequences is demonstrated by the presence of <a id="xref-def-3-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a> and indels affecting target intergenic regions. These rearranged sites, together with genome-wide analyses of <a id="xref-def-3-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a> and indels, will shed light on selection processes shaping intergenic spaces and will likely facilitate the identification of polymorphic markers. In addition, the distribution of <a id="xref-def-3-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a> across <em>S. commersonii</em> genes indicated high variability in genes related to specific biological processes such as macromolecule metabolic processes, response to stimuli, carbohydrate derivative binding, localization, and ion binding.</p><p id="p-47">Our data highlighted a striking difference between <em>S. commersonii</em> heterozygosity (1.5%) and that of the common potato (53 to 59%; <a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Hirsch et al., 2013</a>). Although in nature <em>S. commersonii</em> is an obligate sexually reproducing allogamous species, ex situ maintenance through randomly intercrossing seedlings of accession PI 243503 could have artificially affected its diversity level, causing a reduction in the level of heterozygosity compared with spontaneous populations. By contrast, the high level of potato heterozygosity likely reflects both its vegetative propagation (that fixes heterozygosity) and progress made in the past ∼150 years of concerted potato breeding to maximize heterosis. Since the magnitude of the difference in heterozygosity in <em>S. commersonii</em> relative to <em>S. tuberosum</em> was considerable, fundamental questions arise concerning the genetic constitution and maintenance of wild relatives of potato with regard to gene flow and population structure (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Camadro et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-25-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Hirsch et al., 2013</a>). From a practical perspective, it seems that the use of species-wide diversity rather than individual accessions would be much more desirable to broaden the narrow genetic base of and increase allelic diversity in cultivated potato.</p><p id="p-48">We also found differences between <em>S. commersonii</em> and other solanaceous species in terms of repetitive sequences. Transposable elements (<a id="xref-def-12-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-12">TEs</a>) are major components of all plant genomes studied, shaping genome structure and organization. In a comprehensive review of the first 50 sequenced plant genomes, <a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Michael and Jackson (2013)</a> reported that genome repetitive content ranged from 3% (<em>Utricularia gibba</em>) to 85% (maize [<em>Zea mays</em>]). <a id="xref-def-12-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-12">TEs</a> strongly affect gene expression levels and transcript splicing and consequently may impact plant phenotypes. Compared with potato (55%) and tomato (63%), <em>S. commersonii</em> showed a lower amount of repetitive DNA (∼383 Mb, accounting for 44.5% of the current assembly). As in other solanaceous species, there were many more Ty3-<em>gypsy</em> type than Ty1-<em>copia</em> type <a id="xref-def-4-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">LTR-RTs</a> identified in <em>S. commersonii</em>, suggesting that the former elements have been somewhat more successful in colonizing and persisting in <em>Solanaceae</em> genomes. Moreover, the ratio of Ty3-<em>gypsy</em>:Ty1-<em>copia</em> might also be driven by variation in the efficacy of illegitimate recombination and/or unequal homologous recombination in removing <a id="xref-def-4-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">LTR-RTs</a> from the genomes, as reported in Arabidopsis, maize, barley (<em>Hordeum vulgare</em>), and rice (<em>Oryza sativa</em>; reviewed in <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Bennetzen, 2007</a>). <a id="xref-def-4-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">LTR-RTs</a> play a substantial role in genome size variation, and the lower frequency of <a id="xref-def-12-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-12">TEs</a> in <em>S. commersonii</em> may contribute to its smaller genome size as well as underline the occurrence of different evolutionary dynamics in individual solanaceous species genomes since their separation from a common ancestor. <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Vitte and Bennetzen (2006)</a> suggested that the proportion of <a id="xref-def-12-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-12">TEs</a> in different genomes might be influenced by destabilization of epigenetic regulation.</p><p id="p-49">In our study, we targeted different tissues to best represent the <em>S. commersonii</em> transcript repertoire. The de novo assembly of the transcriptome from leaf, flower, stolon, and tuber tissues allowed identification of 37,662 genes. Even though the number of genes found in <em>S. commersonii</em> was similar to that reported for <em>S. tuberosum</em>, the number of transcripts differed greatly between the two species. This might highlight the presence of more prominent alternative splicing activities in potato than in <em>S. commersonii</em>. This is consistent with observations by the <a id="xref-ref-47-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium (2011)</a> that ∼25% of potato genes encoded two or more isoforms, indicative of more functional variation than is represented by the gene set alone. We also identified ∼21,000 <em>S. commersonii</em> <a id="xref-def-6-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ncRNAs</a>. Emerging evidence has revealed that <a id="xref-def-6-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ncRNAs</a> are major products of the plant transcriptome (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Rymarquis et al., 2008</a>). They may have significant regulatory importance, especially during stress situations (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Matsui et al., 2013</a>). In this study, the perfect or near-perfect match to target sites allowed effective in silico prediction of the target sequences (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Rhoades et al., 2002</a>) and revealed that 10 <a id="xref-def-8-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a> targeted cold-responsive genes. Since manipulation of <a id="xref-def-8-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a>/siRNA-guided gene regulation may enable engineering of plants for improved stress tolerance, detailed analyses on <a id="xref-def-8-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a>-guided stress responsive gene regulation in <em>S. commersonii</em> may lead to new insights for efficient exploitation of this germplasm.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenomic Analysis across Plant Species</h3><p id="p-50">To assess evolutionary relationships between <em>S. commersonii</em> and other sequenced plant genomes, we undertook a comprehensive phylogenomic approach. This involved reconstruction of the complete collection of evolutionary histories of all <em>S. commersonii</em> protein-coding genes across a phylogeny of 12 sequenced plants (i.e., the phylome) and animals (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Huerta-Cepas et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Chipman et al., 2014</a>). The usefulness of this approach in the annotation of newly sequenced genomes has been demonstrated in other plants (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Garcia-Mas et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Dohm et al., 2014</a>). In total, 17,297 (44%) <em>S. commersonii</em> genes showed a one-to-one orthologous relationship with <em>S. tuberosum</em> genes and 16,821 (42%) with <em>S. lycopersicum</em> genes, but only 7058 (18%) with genes from the more distantly related asterid <em>M. guttatus</em> (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Table 12</a>). This scenario, in which most orthology relationships are of the type one-to-many, many-to-many, or many-to-one, likely results from the past genome duplication shared by the three <em>Solanum</em> species (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>), followed by differential loss of paralogous genes in each of the species. The overall average number of duplications per node (duplication density) was 0.66, 0.93, and 0.94 for <em>S. commersonii</em>, potato ancestor, and <em>Solanum</em> ancestor, respectively, whereas we found a low rate of 0.066 for the common ancestor of asterids. Collectively, these numbers suggest multiple rounds of duplications, at least at the lineage preceding the separation of the <em>Solanum</em> ancestor from the other asterid included in this analysis (<em>M. guttatus</em>). These two rounds of ancestral duplications were previously suggested by comparison of the domesticated potato and tomato genomes (<a id="xref-ref-47-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium, 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-59-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>). Our study showed that these ancestral duplications were also shared with the wild species lineage represented by <em>S. commersonii</em>, as would be predicted by commonly accepted <em>Solanum</em> phylogeny. The analysis of paralogous gene pairs revealed that most duplicated genes diverged before the separation of <em>Solanum</em> species. These results are in agreement with the topological dating of duplications, which strongly suggest that these major duplications predate the divergence of the <em>Solanum</em> species and that most paralogous pairs dated as potato-specific or <em>S. commersonii</em>-specific result from differential retention of duplicated pairs in each of the investigated lineages. We assessed the genomic organization of these recent duplicates and found that most were present in tandem (314) or were closely associated along the same contig (141). Thus, results obtained are not compatible with a recent, specific genome duplication in <em>S. commersonii</em> but rather with differential retention of paralogs from ancient duplications and additional lineage-specific segmental duplications that blurred the syntenic and one-to-one correspondence between <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em>. This in turn may underlie the sexual incompatibility between these two related species.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Pathogen-Receptor Genes</h3><p id="p-51">Candidate <em>S. commersonii</em> disease resistance (<em>R</em>) genes were cataloged and compared with the <em>R</em> gene complement comprising the cultivated potato and tomato genomes. Our data revealed that <em>S. commersonii</em> contains fewer <em>R</em> gene candidates than <em>S. tuberosum</em>, but more than tomato. Polyploidization, genome size variation, natural selection, artificial selection including domestication, breeding, and cultivation, and gene family interactions have probably influenced pathogen recognition gene evolution in <em>Solanum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Andolfo et al., 2014b</a>). Differences in copy numbers of specific <em>R</em> gene families are important sources of genetic variation and are likely to play a role in phenotypic diversity and adaptation in different species (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Peele et al., 2014</a>). Our analyses revealed that different R locus arrangements emerged in different species after their separation from a common <em>Solanum</em> ancestor. Indeed, the size of the <em>R1</em> locus varied 10-fold among the genomes analyzed. Previous comparative analysis of the <em>R1</em> locus revealed highly conserved collinear regions that flank sequences showing high variability and tandem duplicated genes (namely, R1 homologs and F-box-containing genes) (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Ballvora et al., 2007</a>). <em>Solanum</em> <em>R</em> gene architecture seems to be shaped by the interplay of large-scale gene organization that determines global conservation of locus order genome wide and extensive local genome rearrangements mediated by tandem duplication, transposons, and other shuffling elements that determine distinct local arrangements (<a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Zhang et al., 2014</a>). Extant local arrangement of <em>Solanum</em> <em>R</em> genes within a genome may be indicative of biological and environmental factors influencing genotype adaptation and may have significant influence on phenotypic resistance diversity. More detailed comparative analyses of <em>R</em> genes within and among <em>Solanum</em> species are warranted.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Nonacclimated and Cold-Acclimated Gene Expression and Regulation</h3><p id="p-52">Previous studies provided evidence that, among potato species, <em>S. commersonii</em> is the most tolerant to low temperatures, with the best capacity to cold acclimate (<a id="xref-ref-44-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Palta and Simon, 1993</a>). The molecular basis of nonacclimated tolerance in potato is poorly understood, although it has been reported that it may be genetically determined by loci independent of acclimated tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Stone et al., 1993</a>). We therefore analyzed the cold-responsive transcriptome of <em>S. commersonii</em> to shed further light on processes that might explain its freezing tolerance and cold acclimation capacity. Overall, whole-genome expression data highlighted an extensive reorganization of the transcriptome under cold stress, with enhanced expression of genes affecting ROS scavenging enzymes (e.g., superoxide dismutase, <em>SOD</em>; catalase, <em>CAT</em>; ascorbate peroxidase, <em>APX</em>), those involved in cell repair (such as <em>HSPs</em> and dehydrins [<em>DHNs</em>]), and those encoding proteins that may function as osmoprotectants. Among the latter, we found a significant upregulation of <em>S. commersonii</em> galactinol synthase (<em>GOLS1</em>) under both <a id="xref-def-10-19" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and <a id="xref-def-9-18" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> conditions. Previously, overexpression of <em>GOLS1</em> from <em>Medicago falcata</em> or <em>Boea hygrometrica</em> promoted the biosynthesis of increased amounts of raffinose family oligosaccharides, such as galactinol, raffinose, and stachyose, and resulted in elevated tolerance to low temperatures in transgenic tobacco (<em>Nicotiana tabacum</em>) plants (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Wang et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Zhuo et al., 2013</a>). Given that, we hypothesize that high expression of <em>S. commersonii GOLS1</em> in conjunction with the increased activity of the aforementioned cold-associated and -inducible proteins might contribute to <em>S. commersonii</em> frost tolerance.</p><p id="p-53">It is notable that several genes were responsive to cold relative to control conditions, but with contrasting kinetics under <a id="xref-def-9-19" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> versus <a id="xref-def-10-20" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>. For instance, <em>BRASSINOSTEROID-SIGNALING KINASE1</em> (<em>BSK1</em>) was activated under <a id="xref-def-9-20" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and suppressed under <a id="xref-def-10-21" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>. Conversely, one MYB and one bHLH <a id="xref-def-11-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TF</a> were cold induced under <a id="xref-def-10-22" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> and repressed under <a id="xref-def-9-21" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a>. As MYB and bHLH proteins often interact with each other to control transcription (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Ramsay and Glover, 2005</a>), this differential expression of MYB and bHLH <a id="xref-def-11-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a> suggests that the regulation of some cold-responsive genes may be achieved by modulating the ratio of these partners. <a id="xref-def-11-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a> were mostly upregulated under both conditions, as was observed in Arabidopsis (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Lee et al., 2005</a>). This is consistent with overall upregulation, rather than repression, of gene transcription following cold stress. Specifically, we identified 25 <a id="xref-def-11-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a> correlating positively with acclimated and nonacclimated tolerance, and only 11 that showed negative correlations. Among the negatively correlating <a id="xref-def-11-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a> was a Cys-2/His-2-type (C2H2) zinc-finger protein (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Sakamoto et al., 2004</a>). C2H2 zinc-finger-type <a id="xref-def-11-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-11">TFs</a> have been found to work downstream of DREB1/CBF and to be responsible for stress tolerance in plants (<a id="xref-ref-52-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Sakamoto et al., 2004</a>).</p><p id="p-54">Our comparison of cold-responsive gene expression profiles between <a id="xref-def-9-22" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a>- and <a id="xref-def-10-23" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>-stressed plants highlighted remarkable features of some of those known to be critical in cold sensing and signaling pathways. Two genes for calcium-dependent protein kinases, <em>CDPK17</em> and <em>CDPK 19</em> (<em>CPK8</em>), were differentially expressed. Interestingly, <em>CDPK19</em> was upregulated only under <a id="xref-def-10-24" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions, whereas <em>CDPK17</em> expression required acclimation. Neither gene has been previously implicated in cold stress response. Thus, the induction of <em>CDPK19</em> (<em>CPK8</em>) and <em>CDPK17</em> in <em>S. commersonii</em> suggests possible independent roles in response to freezing and cold acclimation, respectively. The structural organization and transcriptional activity of the <em>S. commersonii</em> genes for CBFs (C-repeat binding factors) also revealed intriguing features. Our cross-species comparisons indicated that the CBFs underwent rapid expansion via duplication processes. In <em>S. commersonii</em>, we found two pseudogenes, <em>ψCBF2</em> and <em>ψCBF3</em>. Both pseudogenes contain premature stop codons. High identity (100%) between <em>S. commersonii CBF3</em> paralogs suggests that gene duplication occurred in the last ∼2.3 million years, after the divergence between potato and <em>S. commersonii</em> from their most recent common ancestor. In particular, the paucity of sequence change indicates that, after the divergence of the two species lineages, there were likely strong constraints on CBF3 that conserved protein sequence. A different situation was found for <em>S. commersonii ψCBF2</em>, which shares only 80% of identity with the functional <em>S. commersonii CBF2</em>. This suggests that the gene duplication occurred prior to divergence of the <em>S. tuberosum</em> and <em>S. commersonii</em> lineages from their most recent common ancestor, with the duplicated copy subsequently undergoing rearrangements as observed also in other duplicated genes (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Lynch and Force, 2000</a>). Phylogenetic analysis highlighted a common origin of CBFs in <em>Solanum</em> species with respect to other plants from temperate regions that can cold acclimate, such as Arabidopsis, wheat (<em>Triticum aestivum</em>) and <em>Brassica napus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Jaglo et al., 2001</a>). This suggests homogenization mechanisms exist in <em>Solanum</em>, as previously reported (<a id="xref-ref-46-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Pennycooke et al., 2008</a>). Despite observed orthology for most of the CBF1 gene family, <em>S. commersonii CBF1</em> clustered apart from other CBF1 sequences. This might be the result of strong selection pressure toward functional diversification of <em>S. commersonii CBF1</em>. Taken together, our data are consistent with a hypothesis of rapid evolution of CBFs within the genus <em>Solanum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Carvallo et al., 2011</a>). We hypothesize that a duplication event occurred after the <em>S. tuberosum-S. commersonii</em> divergence and may have led to a different functionalization of the <em>S. commersonii CBF3</em>, resulting in enhanced cold response capability in <em>S. commersonii</em>. To more deeply investigate the role of CBFs in <em>S. commersonii</em>, transcript levels were monitored both under <a id="xref-def-9-23" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and <a id="xref-def-10-25" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a>. Our data showed that all <em>S. commersonii</em> CBFs were upregulated relative to controls under all tested conditions. This is in contrast with previous reports that <em>CBF1</em>, but not related CBFs, were responsive to low temperatures in both <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-46-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Pennycooke et al., 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Carvallo et al., 2011</a>). Our observations parallel patterns observed in tomato species. In cold-sensitive cultivated tomato, only <em>CBF1</em> was upregulated in response to cold treatments, whereas in the cold-tolerant wild tomato species <em>Solanum peruvianum</em>, all three CBF genes were cold responsive (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Mboup et al., 2012</a>). High expression of <em>S. commersonii</em> CBF genes and genes regulated by CBF proteins (e.g., COR genes) may be directly responsible for enhanced cold tolerance and acclimation ability in this species.</p><p id="p-55">In conclusion, we report the genome sequence for a wild relative of the cultivated potato <em>S. tuberosum</em>. The genome sequence of <em>S. commersonii</em> is a valuable resource for studying <em>Solanum</em> diversity and improving the cultivated potato. Among significant findings, we identified new cold-regulated genes. The information we generated provides a foundation for further experiments to explore the network of gene regulation required for cold tolerance and acclimation and to determine the function of cold-responsive genes through molecular and cellular approaches. Future challenges include translation of this new knowledge into advances in crop improvement in response to global climate and environment changes. In addition, our work demonstrated the utility of wild crop relative genome sequences in elucidating evolutionary mechanisms contributing to <em>Solanum</em> species diversity. Further sequencing and analysis of crop wild relative genomes will prove crucial to utilization of wild germplasm for improvement of specific traits in crop plants with narrow genetic bases.</p></div></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-14"><h2 class="">METHODS</h2><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Genetic Background of Sequenced Material</h3><p id="p-56">We sequenced the genome of clone cmm1t of <em>Solanum commersonii.</em> It derived from a single seed from accession <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-gen" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=PI243503&amp;atom=%2Fplantcell%2F27%2F4%2F954.atom">PI243503</a> obtained from the Inter-Regional Potato Introduction Station, Sturgeon Bay, Wisconsin (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Figure 2</a>). This clone has been widely characterized and used in our breeding program as a source of resistance genes to biotic and abiotic stresses (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Carputo et al., 1997</a>, <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">2007</a>, <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">2009</a>, <a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">2013</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing and Assembly</h3><p id="p-57">Genomic DNA isolated from leaf material was sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq1000 sequencing platform and assembled using SOAPdenovo (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Luo et al., 2012</a>). Gaps were closed using GapCloser v1.12 (a SOAP suite tool), and sequences shorter than 1000 bp bases were discarded from the final assembly. The gene space of the assembled genome was assessed by aligning <a id="xref-def-2-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">CEGs</a> to the assembly using BLAST with a 65% identity threshold. Reads were aligned to the assembled genome using SOAPaligner v2.21 (a SOAP suite tool) with standard parameters but “-r 0” parameter. We called the <a id="xref-def-3-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNPs</a> by aligning and comparing <em>S. commersonii</em> reads to the assembled <em>S. commersonii</em> genome using SOAPsnp v1.03 (a SOAP suite tool) with “-u” and “-n” options enabled to give better accuracy for heterozygous <a id="xref-def-3-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">SNP</a> detection. Heterozygosity was then calculated by estimating the number of heterozygous calls over the total of the callable bases (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Zheng et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Varshney et al., 2012</a>). We estimated the genome size of <em>S. commersonii</em> using flow cytometry. For more details, see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Repeats, Protein-Coding Genes, and <a id="xref-def-7-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">lncRNA</a> Annotation</h3><p id="p-58">Annotation of repeats and protein coding genes was performed using the MAKER pipeline (v2.27) (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Cantarel et al., 2008</a>) with Illumina mRNA-seq reads obtained from root, stolon, tuber, leaf, and flower tissues and other cDNA read data as supporting evidence. In particular, repeats were annotated with RepeatMasker (v. 3.2.8) using the “Solanaceae” repeats database and with Repeatrunner using a database of <a id="xref-def-12-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-12">TE</a>-encoded proteins included into MAKER installation to help mask repeats that have diverged over time. The repeated fraction was also evaluated by graph-based clustering of repetitive elements in unassembled reads using the RepeatExplorer Web server (<a id="xref-ref-43-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Novák et al., 2013</a>) and by analysis of kmer content using Jellyfish and GCE software (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Liu et al., 2012</a>). Putative SINEs were detected using the SINE-Finder tool and were used to search against published SINE sequences of <em>Solanum tuberosum</em> and other <em>Solanaceae</em> using FASTA (<a id="xref-ref-65-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Wenke et al., 2011</a>; <a href="ftp://ftp.ebi.ac.uk/pub/software/unix/fasta/fasta36/">ftp://ftp.ebi.ac.uk/pub/software/unix/fasta/fasta36/</a>). Members of each family detected in <em>S. commersonii</em> were aligned with MUSCLE (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">Edgar, 2004</a>), and consensus sequences were calculated. Ab initio prediction of protein coding genes was performed using AUGUSTUS (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Stanke and Waack, 2003</a>) and GeneMark (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Lukashin and Borodovsky, 1998</a>). The OrthoMCL pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Li et al., 2003</a>) was used to identify and estimate the number of paralogous and orthologous gene clusters between <em>S. commersonii</em>, <em>S. tuberosum</em>, and <em>Solanum lycopersicum</em>. <a id="xref-def-7-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">lncRNAs</a> were identified using the approach described by <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Boerner and McGinnis (2012)</a>. To distinguish <a id="xref-def-7-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">lncRNA</a> from precursors of other <a id="xref-def-6-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">ncRNA</a>, the set of <a id="xref-def-7-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">lncRNAs</a> was first analyzed with cmscan (e-value 0.01) from Infernal 1.1 against the database of covariate models of Rfam 11.0. Noncoding transcripts were BLAST searched as well against a database of plant mature <a id="xref-def-8-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNA</a> sequences in miRBase (<a href="http://www.mirbase.org/">http://www.mirbase.org/</a>) to identify homologous <a id="xref-def-8-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">miRNAs</a>. For more details, see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3><em>R</em> Gene Analysis</h3><p id="p-59">Matrix-R was used to screen the proteomes of <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> (37,662 and 39,031 proteins, respectively). The set of predicted proteins identified via HMM profiling was further analyzed using Interproscan software version 5.0 (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/pfa/iprscan5/">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/pfa/iprscan5/</a>) to verify the presence of conserved domains and motifs characteristic of R proteins. To identify <em>S. tuberosum</em> R1 orthologs in <em>S. commersonii</em>, we used the data from the orthology relationships. Then, selected homologous sequences were aligned further analyzed using Interproscan software version 5.0 (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/pfa/iprscan5/">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/pfa/iprscan5/</a>) to verify the presence of conserved domains and motifs characteristic of R1 proteins. The CBF protein sequences were aligned using Geneious R6 (Biomatters). The CBF evolutionary history was inferred by the maximum likelihood method using MEGA 6.06 (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Tamura et al., 2011</a>). For more details, see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Cold-Responsive Gene Analysis</h3><p id="p-60">Twelve clonally propagated plants from cmm1t were cultured in a growth chamber under cool white fluorescent lamps (350 to 400 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) at 24°C and then exposed to −2°C for 6 h to test their tolerance to low temperature under <a id="xref-def-10-26" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> conditions. To evaluate cold tolerance following <a id="xref-def-9-24" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a>, six plants were first transferred from a 24°C growth chamber to a cold room (4°C) under cool white fluorescent lamps (100 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) for 2 weeks and then exposed to −2°C for 6 h. RNA expression analysis was performed on a Combimatrix <em>S. tuberosum</em> chip produced by the Plant Functional Genomics Center at the University of Verona. The chip contained 27,234 nonredundant 35-40-mer oligo probes in triplicate. For more details, see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Analysis, Genome Evolution, and Functional Annotation</h3><p id="p-61">The longest protein sequence associated with each predicated <em>S. commersonii</em> gene was used for a Smith-Waterman search against the protein sets of nine other plant species. Alignments were generated and quality-filtered, and phylogenetic trees were calculated for each <em>S. commersonii</em> sequence. A species tree was generated from a super tree of all trees and by multigene phylogenetic analysis of high-confidence 1:1 orthologs. We computed transversion rates at 4-fold degenerate sites (4DTv) as a conservative genetic distance to estimate recent major evolutionary events. To assess divergence between species, individual gene trees in the <em>S. commersonii</em> phylome were scanned to detect one-to-one orthologs between <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. tuberosum</em> and between <em>S. commersonii</em> and <em>S. lycopersicum</em>. To estimate age of duplication waves, we used paralogous gene pairs assigned to the three relevant evolutionary time points. Protein-coding gene predictions were functionally annotated based on protein signatures and orthology relationships. For more details, see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Accession Numbers</h3><p id="p-62">Illumina genome sequences have been deposited in the Sequence Read Archive under study SRP050408, and RNA-seq sequences have been deposited under study SRP050412. The Transcriptome Shotgun Assembly project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under accession number GCHT00000000. The version described in this article is the first version, GCHT01000000. This Whole-Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under accession number JXZD00000000. The version described in this article is version JXZD01000000. Microarray data of <a id="xref-def-9-25" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">AC</a> and <a id="xref-def-10-27" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">NAC</a> experiments are downloadable from <a href="http://ddlab.sci.univr.it/files/scommersonii/results_CNAvsTA_sig.xls">http://ddlab.sci.univr.it/files/scommersonii/results_CNAvsTA_sig.xls</a> and <a href="http://ddlab.sci.univr.it/files/scommersonii/results_TNAvsCNA_sig.xls">http://ddlab.sci.univr.it/files/scommersonii/results_TNAvsCNA_sig.xls</a>, respectively. Phylogenetic trees and alignments are available through PhylomeDB (<a href="http://www.phylomedb.org">http://www.phylomedb.org</a>). Accession numbers and alignment of CBFs are reported in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.135954/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 9</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-22" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-103">This article is dedicated to Luigi Monti for his continuous support and for his ongoing exemplary career in plant genetics and breeding. This work was supported by the Ministry of University and Research (GenHORT Project PON02_00395_3215002) and by a grant from Campania Region (CARINA project POR_FSE 2007/2013). Research of the group of T.G. is also supported by the Spanish Ministry of Economy and Competitiveness (BIO2012-37161), by a grant from the Qatar National Research Fund (NPRP 5-298-3-086), and by a grant from the European Research Council under the European Union's Seventh Framework Programme (FP/2007-2013)/ERC (Grant Agreement ERC-2012-StG-310325). We thank M. Iovene for the cytological analysis, S. Lucretti for the cytometric assays, T. Cardi for producing clone cmm1t, and A. Di Matteo for RNA hybridizations. We also thank N. D’Agostino for kindly reviewing the article.</p></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-2"><h2 class="">AUTHOR CONTRIBUTIONS</h2><p id="p-104">R.A. designed the research, performed experiments, analyzed data, and wrote the article. All authors read and approved the article.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul><li class="fn" id="fn-1"><p id="p-1"><a
